He continued the mission of evangelizing and spreading Christianity throughout the region, laboring successfully for the conversion of the local populations and the strengthening of the church's foundation in what is today Austria and nearby areas.

Vergilius is celebrated for his progressive views on science and education, alongside his evangelizing efforts. He was a scholar and an abbot before becoming a bishop, and he brought with him a deep respect for knowledge and learning. One of his most notable achievements was his argument in favor of the existence of the antipodes, which contradicted the then-prevailing view that the earth was flat and that there could not be inhabited lands on the opposite side of a globe-shaped earth. This position put him in conflict with some church authorities, including St. Boniface himself, but Vergilius was eventually vindicated.

Under Vergilius's leadership, the Diocese of Salzburg became a significant center of learning and missionary activity. He is also credited with founding the monastery of St. Peter in Salzburg, which became a critical center for the spread of Christianity in the region. Through his efforts, many churches and schools were established, further solidifying the Christian faith's presence and influence.

Vergilius's work laid a significant foundation for the Christianization of central Europe, and his legacy is remembered for the blend of faith, reason, and dedication to education and outreach. He died in 784 and was later canonized by the Catholic Church, commemorated as a saint whose feast day is celebrated on November 27th.

Nevertheless, Vergilius ultimately prevailed in this matter. Under his stewardship, the Bishopric of Salzburg grew into a thriving hub for both learning and missionary endeavors. Moreover, he played a pivotal role in the establishment of several religious institutions, such as the Abbey of St. Peter in Salzburg, thereby significantly contributing to the dissemination of the Christian faith throughout the area. As a result of his tireless labor, numerous chapels and schools were founded, further bolstering the foothold of Christianity in these regions. The profound impact of Vergilius's work can still be felt today, particularly in how it set the stage for the eventual Christianization of Central Europe; his legacy lives on through his commitment to the harmonious integration of faith, reason, and educational advocacy.

Despite opposition, notably from St. Boniface, Vergilius' stance was ultimately validated. The Diocese of Salzburg flourished under his guidance, emerging as a prominent hub for learning and missionary enterprise. Among his numerous accomplishments, Vergilius founded the St. Peter Monastery in Salzburg, serving as a crucial vector for Christianity's dissemination. Consequently, numerous churches and schools were erected, reinforcing the Christian faith's stronghold and influence. Vergilius's undertakings paved the way for the Christianization of central Europe, leaving behind a legacy recognized for its fusion of faith, reason, and commitment to education and outreach. Following his demise in 784, Vergilius was canonized by the Catholic Church. His feast day is commemorated annually on November 27th.
